Home &gt; REMARKS AS PREPARED FOR DELIVERY BY MAYOR BUTTIGIEG AT 2016 STATE OF
THE CITY
===============================================================================
***** REMARKS_AS_PREPARED_FOR_DELIVERY_BY_MAYOR_BUTTIGIEG_AT_2016_STATE_OF_THE
CITY [1] *****

**** Introduction ****
Thank you, Chancellor Allison, for that kind introduction, for all of the
extraordinary work that takes place here at IUSB, and for hosting us this
evening in this remarkable facility. Let‚Äôs also thank our talented quartet
once more for that terrific performance.
And thank you to Council President Scott, and to the entire Common Council for
the work you do. Just two months into the year, this Council is already
demonstrating how difficult issues can be handled in an atmosphere of healthy
debate and mutual respect.
This evening we stand near the midpoint of a decade that I believe will go down
in history as the most transformative period for the City of South Bend in
modern times. It‚Äôs also the beginning of a new four-year term of office for
this administration, Clerk, and Council. It is the right moment to take stock
of where we are; and to chart a course for what must happen in the years ahead
of us in order to accelerate and consolidate South Bend‚Äôs historic comeback.
¬ 
**** Review of 2015 ****
Last year alone was full of extraordinary moments in the life of our city. We
spent the year marking our 150th anniversary. An estimated 60,000 people took
part in the Birthday Weekend in May, many them on hand for the inauguration of
River Lights, which will be a permanent legacy of these celebrations. Built
with donated funds and volunteer labor, River Lights has already developed into
a fixture of our downtown, a charismatic work of public art that attracts
diverse visitors from all walks of life and all neighborhoods in town to enjoy
our river across three of our finest parks, right in the heart of the city.¬ 
Last year also saw the culmination of our ambitious effort to tackle the
problem of vacant and abandoned houses by addressing 1,000 houses in 1,000
days. This initiative grew out of complaints and concerns from residents across
town who had spent so many years living next to collapsing structures that they
had begun to question whether anyone cared about their neighborhoods. The
administration and Council heard them, and set out to address the problem
strategically. I am proud to report that when the clock ran out on 1,000 days,
we had addressed 1,122 properties. And 1,000 houses did not mean 1,000
demolitions. In fact, 433 of these houses were repaired, not torn down, through
a combination of effective code enforcement and strong community partnerships.
Meanwhile, the Group Violence Intervention entered its second year, aiming to
make South Bend a safer city by using proven methods to tackle group- and gang-
related gun violence. Too many times, a violent incident is followed by a
community meeting of residents up in arms, a resolution to do better‚Ä¶ and a
return to doing exactly what we were doing before. But after a painful summer
in 2012, we made sure that this time really was different. Community leaders
sought out the best, most effective approaches from around the country, and
began to apply them.
The results are early and tentative, but they are very promising. We are seeing
changes in the patterns of group- and gang-member involved shooting incidents
in our City, and already we have seen over 30 community members on the path to
contributing to society in a job rather than harming others. The data suggest
that we are already saving lives and have the potential to do much more,
through our ongoing effort and a new, dedicated GVI unit in our Police
Department.
Just as importantly, through this effort we have built a coalition of
foundation leadership, social service providers, neighborhood representatives,
respected pastors, nonprofit stakeholders, police officers and local, county,
and federal law enforcement officials now working in concert toward a simple
goal that everyone shares regardless of background or politics: violence must
stop.¬ 
On the economic front, the banner news of 2015 came just in time for Christmas,
when Governor Pence visited our region to celebrate our success in the
competitive Regional Cities program, earning an award of $42 million from the
Indiana Economic Development Corporation. We have come a long way from the
years when South Bend complained of having little to no state economic
development activity in our city limits.
The Regional Cities program was designed to invite communities to learn how to
collaborate regionally, because there is powerful evidence that neighboring
communities that simply compete against each other are defeating themselves.
This time, we saw communities throughout St. Joseph, Marshall, and Elkhart
Counties‚Äîplaces that used to view each other as competitors‚Äîcome together
and offer a shared economic vision for our future. And as a team, we won.
By teaming up with our neighbors‚Äîwith public, private, and university
leadership‚Äîwe earned funding to support an estimated $700 million in total
investment for projects ranging from the Renaissance District on old Studebaker
grounds, to the Blueways Trail that will deepen our community‚Äôs bond with our
magnificent river and make one of our great parks even greater. In a matter of
weeks, we‚Äôll know for sure whether the Indiana General Assembly will fully
fund this program. Two-thirds of the funding has already been secured; the
remainder is currently hanging in the balance of the legislative process. I
have contacted our legislators and the Speaker of the House to emphasize the
importance of this program to our region, tomorrow I will travel to the
Statehouse to testify in the Ways and Means Committee in favor of full funding.
We are hoping for good news from Indianapolis soon. ¬ 
Last year‚Äôs successes closed out a phenomenal four-year term for the
administration and outgoing Council. Over those four years, we saw unemployment
fall by more than half from 11.7 percent to 5.5 percent, with 2873 net new jobs
created over that period and a steady labor force.¬  We‚Äôve seen nearly a
half-billion dollars in private investment on city-supported projects over that
period. Our neighborhoods improved, while downtown saw a boom in dining and
retail establishments. Meanwhile population estimates suggest that the City
experienced its largest one-year increase in nearly a quarter-century, pointing
the way to further growth before the 2020 Census.
¬ 
**** Mission Statement and Three Lines of Effort ****
Everyone who has had a hand in the last few years‚Äô successes should be proud
of what we have achieved together. But we know that much work remains, and
tonight I would like to focus on the future. Not just the plans and programs,
but the approach and the values that will guide our work.
The mission statement for our administration is straightforward: we deliver
services that empower everyone to thrive.
Behind these eight words‚Äî‚ÄúWe deliver services that empower everyone to
thrive‚Äù‚Äîis a deeply held philosophy about why our city government exists in
the first place.
It is based on the idea of thriving. That can mean different things for
different people, but whatever it means to you, you are better able to thrive
if you live in a city that does its work well. That's why cities were formed,
going back to the first settlements millennia ago: human beings learned that we
have a better chance of thriving when we live in a community.
In our mission statement, the word ‚Äúeveryone‚Äù means everyone. It's not
enough if only some have a chance to thrive here. If prosperity is growing but
some are not able to participate, then we have our work cut out for us.
It's not up to us in city government to single-handedly cause everyone to
thrive. But we can help empower everyone to thrive. We can tear down the
obstacles that affect people's ability to thrive. When you are part of a
community that functions well, you are better empowered to live a life of your
choosing.
And everything we do to make that happen comes down to one word: service. That
is the thing we provide, every day and night, in order to make that these goals
can happen. It is what we do, day and night. Everything we offer, from a police
patrol to raft ride down the East Race, from a snow plow in the winter to an
emergency cooling center in the summer; it‚Äôs a service that exists to empower
everyone to thrive.
To meet this mission, we focus on three lines of effort, and if you work for
the City you've heard me talk about this before. First, we make the basics
easy. Second, we deliver good government. And third, we invest in people and
places.
These three lines of effort have defined our work up to today, and I‚Äôd like
to say more about how they will drive our work on the road ahead.
First, making the basics easy. For us, this means that the things you shouldn't
even have to think about are handled for you by a capable City. You‚Äôre not
empowered to thrive if snow is blocking your ability to get out of your
neighborhood. You‚Äôre not empowered to thrive if there's no one to come to
your rescue in a medical emergency. You‚Äôre not empowered to thrive if there
isn't clean, safe drinking water available on demand. Our goal is that the
basics of life are so easy that residents don't even realize we‚Äôre doing this
work--even though the work is never easy.
We‚Äôve just seen a tragic reminder of what happens when you can‚Äôt take the
basics for granted. Last week, I had the opportunity to spend time with the new
mayor of Flint, Michigan and discuss her efforts to lead a community forward
after it was literally poisoned by unsafe water. What happened in her city is a
reminder of how completely residents rely on cities to provide for basic needs.
It‚Äôs why our water works meets all of the Environmental Protection Agency‚Äôs
standards and regulations and it‚Äôs why our staff performs thousands of tests
to ensure safe drinking water, sourced from the Hilltop and the Saint Joseph
aquifers.
It‚Äôs not just water. It‚Äôs trash pickup, sewer works, and a hundred other
things we just count on getting done. It‚Äôs why we established the 311
service, which took over 142,000 calls last year. With an average wait time
below 20 seconds, 311 has handled everything from traffic signal requests to
animal control issues.
It‚Äôs why the Fire department spends time not only putting out fires but
preventing them. Last year the Fire Department provided hundreds of hours of
public education, installed hundreds of smoke and carbon monoxide alarms to
homeowners, and had contact with thousands of local children to stress the
importance of prevention in their homes and schools.
Going into 2016 and beyond, we will continue to work to make the basics easy so
that residents are empowered to focus on living the life of their choosing.
The infrastructure of our city‚Äîmuch of it unseen‚Äîremains a major area of
focus for this administration, with many improvements to come. As the world‚Äôs
first city to manage our waste water systems in the digital cloud, we will
continue to focus on managing what‚Äôs underground well, especially when it
comes to our federally mandated plan to separate City sewers to comply with the
clean water act. With hundreds of millions of dollars of spending required, we
will be using every tool available to us‚Äîtechnical, legal, and political‚Äîto
minimize the impact this work will have on ratepayers.
Meanwhile, a form of infrastructure we never used to think about‚Äîdigital
infrastructure‚Äîpresents a new challenge but also an opportunity to move South
Bend ahead of the rest. Thanks to a partnership with the leading community
benefactors, we are just weeks away from offering free Wi-Fi downtown through a
wireless mesh that will be expandable and usable in ways we haven‚Äôt even
fully imagined yet. I can‚Äôt wait to cut the ribbon‚Äîor perhaps cut the
Ethernet cable‚Äîon that important project to help accelerate downtown growth
and expand access.
When it comes to the most visible part of our infrastructure‚Äîgood old-
fashioned roads and streets‚Äîwe are closely watching developments in the
General Assembly in Indianapolis. I have crossed party lines to support the
road funding bill recently passed in the House, because I believe it represents
the best chance we‚Äôve had in years to ease our reliance on a shrinking gas
tax and fund our badly needed road improvements for the years to come.
If the first line of effort for meeting our mission is to make the basics easy
for residents, the second is to deliver good government. Good government means
putting residents first always, leading by example, gathering input and
transparently communicating our intentions, decisions, and actions.
It‚Äôs why we use 311 not just as a place residents can call, but as a source
of data for relentless performance improvement. It‚Äôs why we ask the City‚Äôs
new Chief Innovation Officer and Chief Technology Officer not just to fix
computers around the County-City building, but to develop strategies for using
technology to enhance transparency and empower residents. That‚Äôs why we care
so much about the awards South Bend received from the Government Finance
Officers of America for excellence in financial reporting and in budget
presentation‚Äîone of just two cities in Indiana to receive this level of
recognition for the soundness and transparency of our financial reporting.
It‚Äôs not about the books; it‚Äôs about using the books to empower residents
to hold their government accountable.
It‚Äôs why we were among the first to put checkbook-level data on all City
spending online for anyone to see. It‚Äôs what‚Äôs at stake in things like the
legal department‚Äôs handling of requests for information under the Access to
Public Records Act. They delivered 1,788 records last year alone, bringing the
total of this administration to 7,913‚Äîall without one finding of violation by
the Public Access Counselor.
Good government also means fiscal stewardship. Once again last year, the City
maintained our AA bond rating. Only 5 second-class cities in all of Indiana
have bond ratings at least this good‚Äîand the other four are wealthy
Indianapolis suburbs. That bond rating helped our finance team save taxpayers
$5.6 million last year alone by re-financing city bonds.
Meanwhile, our focus on safety has led to a workers‚Äô compensation cost that
is more than a third less than the state average. Making sure our workers are
healthy and safe isn‚Äôt just the right thing to do as an employer‚Äîit‚Äôs
also part of fiscal stewardship. That‚Äôs why last month we opened an employee
wellness clinic, which will not only benefit the City employees and family
members treated there, but all taxpayers, since it will help us to slow the
rise of employee health care costs.
Maybe the simplest way to see what the drive for good government looks like is
to visit the Code Enforcement department‚Äôs offices. In 2012, if you walked
into that office, paper was literally stacked so high that you couldn‚Äôt see
to the other side. Countless thousands of files cluttered the office and took
enormous time to physically search. We are now in the midst of a complete
transformation, and while I could quote you the numbers on what the use of
technology has meant in that department, the impact is best seen by simply
looking around at an office you can actually walk around in without tripping
over files, where hard-working inspectors are finally closing more cases than
we have opened in the last two years.
But we have to do even more to make sure that every resident has access to the
same information that administration officials have so they can participate in
an informed way in public debates. That means even more machine-readable data
on our open data portal, and updating the website to make it easier to navigate
and find city information later this year.
Meanwhile, to make sure the engine of government is in tune, we will continue
to refine our budget practices, including making our budget presentations even
more user-friendly. We will also begin to implement priority-based budgeting, a
new approach that will help use our scarce dollars based on what we most need
to achieve.
I am proud of our continued fiscal strength, but that fiscal strength is the
sum total of a thousand tough decisions, and there will be more ahead. In
particular, a looming fiscal cutoff in 2020 means we need to prepare now‚Äîas
early as the 2017 budget‚Äîby ensuring that our services are efficient and our
spending is prioritized.
This isn‚Äôt like the drastic fiscal cliff that Mayor Luecke‚Äôs administration
faced in 2008, but it is enough of a hit that we need to prepare. We will face
a shortfall currently estimated at nearly $7 million, and I will be working
with Council beginning this year to make sure we are dealing with this issue
well ahead of time to spare future pain.
I‚Äôve spoken of making sure the basics are easy and delivering good
government, two pillars of how we meet our mission to provide services that
empower everyone to thrive. Our third line of effort is to invest in people and
places. If the fundamentals of city government are there to make life here
possible, these investments are about helping make life here great.
Investing in places has continued to accelerate our City‚Äôs comeback. Last
year we saw 907 jobs created through the efforts of our Department of Community
Investment, drawing $164 million in private investment to our City.
And there‚Äôs more where that came from. Every day out my office window, I see
renovation work on the Chase Tower, a $31 million investment that will bring
183 new hotel rooms and 80 apartments to the heart of our city. There is a lot
of work to do, but we are expecting the lights to be on once again in the Chase
Tower in 2017.
That hotel will soon be joined by a five-story, 139-room hotel at the site of
the former College Football Hall of Fame, a Courtyard Marriott to be connected
with the Century Center by underground tunnel. This may sound like more
competition for existing hotels, but the reality is that everyone can gain from
access to new markets, competing for convention business that used to ignore us
because we did not have enough hotel rooms.
This year will see the biggest wave yet in what will eventually be hundreds of
new units of housing downtown. Construction is already underway on the former
LaSalle Hotel, Hoffman Hotel, JMS building, Hill and Colfax site, and former
Transpo Site, and more is forthcoming along the East Bank and River Race areas.
Investing in great places also means ensuring our City‚Äôs corridors are
something to be proud of. The West Side Main Streets corridor plan brought over
five million dollars in city investment to the West Side, with major
streetscape improvements to Lincoln Way West and Western Avenues, corridors
that have needed attention for years. These improvements came after an
extensive process of public engagement, featuring neighborhood stakeholder
committees and over a dozen public meetings. The move had its skeptics, but now
that the results are in, residents and businesses along the corridors are
already asking for more. A new fa√ßade matching grant program leveraged a total
investment of over $465,000 along Western Avenue and Lincoln Way West, with
more applications pending for the year to come.
I will admit that there has been a sacrifice involved: the commute on Lincoln
Way became longer. To get from downtown to the airport just outside the city
limits at rush hour now takes seven minutes, according to the South Bend
Tribune. I believe it used to take six and a half minutes. I also believe the
benefit was worth the cost for a streetscape that is safer for pedestrians and
drivers, and more supportive of small businesses along the way.
Every area where we have implemented Smart Streets, from William and Lafayette
downtown, to Jefferson on the East Side, and Lincoln Way West and Western on
the West Side, has seen skeptics become supporters once they see the results.
But the biggest transformation of all will take place in 2016.
This year will see the majority of the downtown phase of Smart Streets
completed, from Chippewa in the South to Bartlett in the North, as we convert
Michigan, Main, and St. Joseph Streets to a 2-way pattern including center turn
lanes and partial protected bike lanes, and introduce roundabouts in strategic
places to enhance safety and traffic flow.
I want to be very honest with the public about this: there will be disruption
in 2016 as a result of this project. Beginning this spring, traffic will flow
in reduced lanes, and for about three months in late summer, the intersections
of Michigan at Bartlett Street and Marion Streets will close entirely, except
for access to the hospital. This means that commuters will have to temporarily
find new routes and/or deal with an increase in commute time during this
project. Given a choice of spreading this effort over years versus getting it
done quickly, we opted to ‚Äúrip off the band-aid‚Äù as quickly as possible.
The inconvenience will be real and significant. But I am firmly convinced that
the end result will be a better streetscape, a better downtown, and ultimately
a better South Bend.
Investing in people and places also means investing in neighborhoods. We have
continued to support neighborhood quality of life enhancements like curbs and
sidewalks, installing 34,019 feet of new sidewalk and 13,927 feet of new curbs,
and adding 189 ramps to make intersections wheelchair-accessible.
We planted 821 trees last year in the course of our place-making projects as
part of a number of projects to invest in great places around our city, and the
Light Up South Bend program completed its latest phase. Driven by Council
Members Tim Scott and Karen White in partnership with the administration and
Indiana Michigan Power, Light Up South Bend added 70 new lights to the
Southeast Side, building on previous work in LaSalle Park and Keller Park
neighborhood.
And, of course, we are investing in our treasured Parks and Recreation system.
We are now in year three of a five-year master plan, and we‚Äôre deploying
resources from a $5 million parks bond approved last year. Most of the work is
still to come, but already we have upgraded playground equipment at Boland
Park, initiated landscaping improvements at Pinhook, brought new concessions to
Leeper Park‚Äôs Tennis Courts and installed new LED lighting on our river walk.
Major projects ahead include improvements to the Charles Black Center, the
Howard Park Ice Rink, Kelly Playground, Kennedy Park, Fremont Park, and a new
Aerial Adventure Park at Rum Village. Along our river, the former Notre Dame
boathouse will be converted for use by the community, and we will undertake
many less visible but very important investments in maintenance needs like air
conditioners and boilers.
I am thrilled that we will soon be able to deliver these improvements. But the
truth is that there is much more we can and should do, and I will be asking our
Parks and Recreation staff and Board to evaluate the potential for a second
parks bond to carry these investments even further.
Speaking of parks, as you may have seen in the media, there is considerable
community interest in the future of Elbel Golf Course and the surrounding
wetlands. I appreciate the Common Council‚Äôs decision help make sure this
issue gets looked at from all angles. While nothing has been decided yet about
the future, I will say that any resolution will have to meet several goals,
including: maintaining golf at least in the medium term, protecting the nearby
wetlands, accommodating public access, and reducing the drain on City funds.
Moving forward, I expect the process to be exemplary in its inclusion of
diverse community voices and perspectives so we can find the best solution for
the community going forward.
The big picture for our parks and recreation system is this: we have limited
resources but unlimited imagination when it comes to making sure these great
public spaces contribute to active lifestyles and public health, economic
growth, inclusive recreation, preservation of green space, and distinctive
quality of place. Everything we do with parks and recreation will be in
furtherance of these goals, building toward a twofold vision: first, everyone
should be able to enjoy a park near their home, and secondly, the system should
feature unique spaces that the whole City is proud of. I have every aspiration
and expectation that this administration will leave our city‚Äôs network of
parks and public spaces better than we found it.
Not only are we investing in parks, but we are also preparing to reimagine the
way our key assets are managed. The City has an array of resources that enhance
quality of life in the city, including 60 parks and recreation assets,
facilities like the Century Center, the Morris Performing Arts Center and
Palais Royale (which, by the way, just won the prestigious League of American
Theaters 2015 Outstanding Theater Award), and several parking garages‚Äîplus
terrific partner organizations like DTSB.
Now is the right time for us to make sure all these great assets work in
harmony with each other, with a shared vision and better opportunities for
collaboration and specialization across these areas for the benefit of the
City. I will be asking our Parks Superintendent and former DTSB director, Aaron
Perri, to take the lead in ensuring these resources are managed as part of a
holistic vision for the quality of life of our community, and we will keep the
Council updated on any organizational adjustments we feel are needed in order
to make the most of these community treasures.
Crucially, we don‚Äôt only invest in places. We‚Äôre mindful that investing in
people can be just as important, and economic development increasingly means
making sure we have a workforce of residents ready to take advantage of the
economic opportunities to come. That‚Äôs why the SMART workforce program offers
sector-based training to help workers prepare for opportunities, placing 26 of
the 39 initial graduate into new employment already.
Last summer I had the opportunity to tour a vegetable garden with a hundred
4th-graders from Kennedy school. That was different example of investing in
people‚Äîinvesting in our kids. And thanks to the leadership of the St. Joseph
County Health Department and the Reducing Obesity Coalition of St. Joseph
County, working with the City, Schools, and other community partners, we were
recognized by First Lady Michelle Obama as the first city in Indiana to meet
all five goals of the ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs Move!‚Äù initiative for healthy kids.
I believe that the future of development in cities will be focus on investing
in people at least as much as we invest in real estate. From workforce
development efforts to new partnerships with our school corporation, we will
continue to seek out ways to do just that during the year and years to come.
¬ 
**** Five core values for the administration ****
These three major areas of focus‚Äîmaking the basics easy, delivering good
government, and investing in people and places‚Äîwill continue to animate
everything we do. Competing priorities will be tested based on how well they
meet one or more of these lines of effort in support of our mission‚Äîto
deliver services that empower everyone to thrive‚Äîbecause that is why we are
here.
But it's not just what we do‚Äîit's how we do business that matters. To guide
the years ahead, I would like to focus on five values that define what kind of
administration‚Äîand community‚Äîwe are.
In all our work, we will be pursuing the values of excellence, accountability,
innovation, inclusion, and empowerment. We will communicate constantly about
this so that by the end of this year, every City employee at every level to be
able to describe what these values mean to them.
Excellence, to us, means setting a goal for everything that this administration
does to be the best in the State of Indiana, and/or in the top 25% of the
country. We will relentlessly measure and evaluate where we stand to see if we
are the best in the state. When we are, we will work to stay that way. When
we‚Äôre not, we will build plans to move in that direction.
I‚Äôve spoken earlier about our City‚Äôs excellence when it comes to financial
management, smart infrastructure, and public places. We have also been
recognized for excellence in the way we do business. Recently we received a
national award from the NewDEAL Network and Governing Magazine for using data
to engage neighborhoods, and just yesterday the National Association of Clean
Water Agencies recognized the City with the Public Service Award and the
Operations and Environmental Performance Award.‚Äîthe only city to receive two
National Environmental Achievement Awards from that organization this year.
Sometimes excellence comes in a dimension that doesn‚Äôt win awards, but
matters hugely to our most vulnerable. When the South Bend Animal Care and
Control increased the live transfer rate for dogs thanks to their good work and
partnerships with volunteers and organizations like Care of South Bend, the
euthanasia rate fell well below the average for open admission shelters. I
don‚Äôt know if there are awards for that sort of thing, but I do know it means
a lot to the four-legged community.
Excellence is why we will spend much of this year updating our performance
management to make sure we are tracking progress, reporting problems, and
celebrating success in our city team.
Next to excellence comes the value of accountability. Accountability means we
put residents first always, offering services at the greatest value to the
taxpayer, with clear and transparent indications of how the government is using
public resources. We want all of our intentions and actions to be clear, and we
expect to be held accountable for the results.¬ 
Accountability means that everyone in this administration must step up and take
ownership of our outcomes and the way we get there. That‚Äôs true for internal
management, and for how we engage the public.
We are also ensuring that accountability is a tool for building confidence in
law enforcement. This trust will grow as we make more data publicly available.
For example, our police chief wants the community to know that instances of the
use of force are down 10% from a year ago, and that 99.97% arrests do not
involve uses of force. To build trust, we need to put the data‚Äîgood, bad, or
indifferent‚Äîout for the community to see, and I look forward later this year
to announcing new tools that will help us to do exactly that.
Not only do residents hold us accountable; they turn to the city‚Äôs law and
code enforcement to hold others accountable when they affect their neighbors‚Äô
ability to thrive. It is a source of pride that last year over 200 vacant and
abandoned homes were ultimately repaired privately, by the owner, as a result
of enforcement action without use of taxpayer funds. At the same time, we have
worked to be reasonable, using tools like cash performance bonds and repair
agreements in code enforcement to work with owners willing and able to make
their properties safe again.
Our legal department has also driven accountability by increasing collection
rates on outstanding fines and fees‚Äînot to be more punitive, but to make sure
these costs do not wind up unfairly falling on the rest of the taxpayers of
this community. By more than doubling EMS collections, the department of law
has helped our Fire Department gather needed funds to keep residents safe. By
tripling code enforcement collections, they have protected neighborhoods from
unsafe buildings. And by more than doubling collections on property damage,
they have made sure that ordinary taxpayers do not bear the burden of harm to
city equipment.
An upcoming landlord registration bill in Council will further enhance
accountability by ensuring that the City has a point of contact for every
property we encounter. This isn‚Äôt just about finding irresponsible landlords;
it‚Äôs about making sure we are able to stay in touch with the majority of
landlords who do the right thing.
And our Human Rights Commission makes sure we have a culture of accountability
when it comes to the city‚Äôs commitment to treating everyone equally. Handling
over 5,000 inquiries last year alone, the Human Rights Commission has done
remarkable work to ensure that everyone can count on being treated equally and
with respect when it comes to public accommodation in our community.
Going forward, we will continue to stress accountability, internally and
externally, with a particular focus on making sure that residents have the
tools to hold us accountable for the quality of service we provide.
The value of innovation means that we deliver better services ever more
efficiently by introducing creative approaches to government operations,
questioning habit and using evidence to continuously improve. When I ask why we
do things a certain way, there‚Äôs only one truly wrong answer: ‚Äúwe do it
this way because we‚Äôve always done it this way.‚Äù
The South Bend Office of Innovation has helped put our City on the national map
when it comes to creative problem-solving. In September, South Bend was one of
two cities featured at the White House to help announce the MetroLab network, a
national collaboration featuring 22 city-university partnerships that advance
the research, development, and deployment of smart solutions to urban problems.
I‚Äôm looking forward to future results from our collaboration with the
University of Notre Dame on this important initiative.
Practicing the value of innovation is a big part of how our Sustainability team
has made our city more environmentally responsible, helping up to 16 city
facilities establish free recycling, and helping to install a rooftop solar
demonstration at Century Center. The team‚Äôs work was even recognized by the
EPA for diverting waste and ensuring recycling at the SB150 celebration
weekend.
Sometimes innovation builds upon itself; after the Council passed an innovative
food truck ordinance, our team created an online tracker that shows on a
dynamic map where the food trucks are actually located. We're working on
another online tool to visualize vacant lots, so residents and potential
investors or nonprofits can find ways to research and evaluate vacant land in
the City.
Innovation isn‚Äôt something we do for its own sake. It helps us serve
residents better‚Äîlike fixes to our trash system that cut missed trash pickups
in half and led to a 75% reduction in solid waste complaints thanks to better
management and use of 311 data. And it saves on the bottom line for taxpayers,
saving $85,000 last year by using cleaner-burning natural gas in our city
fleet, or an innovative energy efficiency contract at Century Center that will
save nearly $8 million over 15 years.
Next, I‚Äôd like to discuss the value of inclusion. That means we ensure the
City administration, both as an employer and as a purchaser, reflects the
community we serve and includes diverse voices in our decision-making and
actions. We often talk about diversity in city government, but there is more to
the story than that. Diversity has to do with who is in the room. Inclusion has
to do with making sure those different voices in the room truly are heard.
We‚Äôve been working on this from day one, and making progress. As an employer,
we have had initiatives like the successful Women‚Äôs Leadership Conference for
networking and idea-sharing among female employees. Departments like our Fire
Department have made attracting a diverse talent pool a priority. The results
are impressive: the latest pool had 512 applicants, including 34% minority and
female applicants. Meanwhile, the Police Department is already underway on
training in Fair and Impartial Policing, Procedural Justice, Reconciliation,
and Implicit Bias.
As a purchaser, we have worked under the Diversity Utilization ordinance to
conduct city-wide training and data collection on the inclusion of women and
minority business enterprises in the city‚Äôs bid and quote process.
These efforts count, but like every diverse city in America that is being
honest with itself right now, we know we can do more. That‚Äôs why this year,
on Dr. King‚Äôs birthday, I signed an executive order establishing the overall
policy for Diversity and Inclusion that will guide me and our leadership team
during my second term. The order makes it the policy of the city to
‚Äúchallenge itself to become a model of equal opportunity, diversity, and
inclusion in its workplace and through its procurement of goods and
services.‚Äù
A more diverse and inclusive environment is not the work of any one person or
one committee‚Äîit is the responsibility of every single member of our
administration, and indeed every member of our community. The executive order
calls on every city department to play a role, and every city policy to come
under consideration, as we establish a proactive plan to be delivered later
this year that will guide our actions in every department over the next four
years and beyond.
The new Diversity and Inclusion Officer will lead this process. We are
interviewing finalists and hope to announce this new city leader shortly.
Having a full-time professional expert at expanding diversity and inclusion
will make us more effective in living up to this value in an administration
that truly reflects the community we serve.
We do this not to check a box or to look good, but because it makes us all
better at our jobs. Research shows that even private companies with diverse
executive teams financially outperform less diversely-led companies. I can see
it in our own work. Having a Department of Law that is a regional model for
diversity and inclusion is, I am convinced, part of why the Corporation Counsel
has delivered such extraordinary results. Having one of the most diverse
mayor‚Äôs office staffs in the state doesn‚Äôt just make us a more inclusive
team, it makes me a better mayor.
Last but certainly not least is empowerment. For us that means ensuring we have
a work environment that enables employees to contribute richly to the
administration and the community, taking pride and ownership in their work.
Many great ideas in City government lately have come up the ranks, not from the
top down, and we aim to continue that.
Some of the most memorable events I‚Äôve attended‚Äîfrom a Veteran‚Äôs Day
event for city employees to a program that saves the lives of un-adopted pets
by coaching juveniles in detention to train them to overcome behavior
issues‚Äîcame from the ranks of our police officers. Our code enforcement
officers have continued to take asbestos certification training, growing their
skill set while saving taxpayer money on contractors. And as we speak, there
are billboards across town celebrating Black History Month thanks to a
partnership that started with an idea from the administrative assistant in my
office.
Empowering employees is one reason the City is in the first of a three-year
plan to elevate the minimum wage we pay our workers to $10.10. In real dollars,
that‚Äôs what the federal minimum wage would be if it just kept up with
inflation since it was introduced in 1968. I know we are not the only local
employer to see this as important. We want to lead by example, and I hope more
employers in the community join us in elevating the standard of living of our
people so that they are empowered to bring their best selves to work every day.
Empowering employees is part of the ‚Äúhow;‚Äù empowering residents is the
‚Äúwhy.‚Äù We thrive when ours is a community of residents empowered to live
the life of their choosing.
That‚Äôs why we continue to pursue a municipal ID program that empowers
everyone who calls South Bend to become a card-carrying member of our City and
use it as a way to say they live here and prove who they are. The Council
authorized the budget for this program, and we are working through logistical
and legal issues to bring it forward soon.
And it‚Äôs why we have answered President Obama‚Äôs call and joined the White
House‚Äôs ‚ÄúMy Brother‚Äôs Keeper‚Äù initiative, setting out to empower young
men and boys of color to live better lives through an emphasis on early
childhood education and neighborhood violence reduction.
We‚Äôre working with some of the most at-risk youth in our community through
the Made program at the Martin Luther King Center, empowering local teens to
make better choices in their lives.
We‚Äôre working to empower residents to continue living in their homes, thanks
to the South Bend Home Improvement Program and Rebuilding Together, which
combined contributed to more than 62 owner-occupied home repairs last year for
low-income and senior residents. Meanwhile South Bend Mutual homes is
empowering low-income residents to take charge of their financial future while
living in a new co-operative housing development, right where vacant and
abandoned housing used to stand on the West Side.
We‚Äôre even using empowerment as a strategy in law enforcement. Our police
don‚Äôt only track investigations and arrests; they track how many positive
contacts there are with members of the community, including 450 contacts made
through community policing, 413 kids served through the Police Athletic League,
and over 1000 participants in Police-sponsored Cinco de Mayo and Dia de los
Reyes events in the last year.
And we will do everything we can to empower our kids to succeed in life, which
is why I am closely following the School Board‚Äôs search for a new
superintendent, and will continue to seek out ways the City can be a supportive
partner to our independent School Corporation.
¬ 
**** Values at work in the community ****
These aren‚Äôt just administration values. They capture something about South
Bend at large. Every day I see some example of how these values play out in our
community. And I see a City that is ready to meet future challenges to live
these values in a meaningful way.
Excellence as I have defined it‚Äîbeing at or near the top in the state or
indeed the country‚Äîhas been on display across this City, last year and every
year. Whether it‚Äôs unique moments like the South Bend Cubs earning the John
H. Johnson Award for being the best club in all of Minor League Baseball, or
annual events like the Fischoff continuing its work as the world‚Äôs leading
chamber music competition; whether it‚Äôs the Community Foundation-led ‚ÄúGive
Local‚Äù campaign raising more nonprofit donations than almost any other
location in the country last May‚Äî$6.7 million in one day‚Äîor the globally
recognized performances of the Toradze Piano studio whose home is right in this
building‚Äîour community is full of people doing extraordinary things that mark
them among the best in the country or even the world.
Our community is full of innovation. A group of young artists taking over an
empty mansion and then the basement of a former power plant to create
distinctive local art‚Äîthat‚Äôs innovation. So is Make South Bend, a local
outpost of the maker movement that creates and markets recreated and assembled
products, or the social enterprise Angels‚Äô Wings, which helps low income
people get to work through ride sharing. It‚Äôs everywhere from the high-tech
startups of Innovation and Ignition Park to the pay-it-forward coffee shop in
the Near Northwest Neighborhood.
The spirit of inclusion is rich in our community, exemplified by the phenomenal
work done at the Taste of Peace, or Martin Luther King Jr. Day. It is lived by
the local interfaith organizations that reach across religious boundaries at a
time when intolerance has grown to a frightening fever-pitch in the national
presidential campaign. It is lived by local faith-based and nonprofit groups
that help immigrant and refugee families live up to their potential as some of
our most productive community members‚Äînot as scapegoats in political theater.
It is lived by those who put stickers in the windows of their businesses at a
time when our state legislature had the whole country wondering if Indiana is a
welcoming place, saying yes, South Bend is an open City.
And when it comes to empowerment‚Äîwell in a way that‚Äôs what South Bend does
best. I‚Äôve seen it for myself. I‚Äôve seen 7th graders from some of our most
at-risk neighborhoods learning to build websites through the South Bend Code
School. I‚Äôve seen low-income residents getting needed loans through a
financial inclusion organization created and staffed by undergraduates, and
I‚Äôve seen a school principal collaborating with professional artists,
volunteers, and kids to completely transform the face of Perley Fine Arts
Academy with a participatory mural. Every day from my desk I see a crane
building a Children‚Äôs Hospital that will empower families to live better
lives because they will no longer have to travel to Chicago or Indianapolis for
specialized pediatric services.
I‚Äôve seen local faith leaders assemble a truckload of water to go to Flint,
Michigan and help with the water crisis; and I‚Äôve seen neighborhood youth
overcome challenges to perform Shakespeare like professional thespians.
Every day, you see these values displayed in our community, by people leading
organizations and people leading ordinary lives.
Not everything in the next year, or the next four years, will come easy. It
will take hard work, focus, discipline, and‚Äîyes‚Äîvigorous and healthy debate
over exactly how we move ahead.
Occasionally we have been questioned for moving too quickly. I understand where
that comes from, and I recognize that sometimes, you have to tap the brakes and
make sure of where we‚Äôre going. But the successes of these recent years show
that we are on the right track.
If we look like we‚Äôre in a hurry, it‚Äôs because there is not a moment to
lose. The future is getting brighter by the day, just like the accelerating
return of sunlight that comes to us every year in February and March.
The first half of this decade proved that South Bend is alive and growing, and
SB150 established once and for all that South Bend is back. Let‚Äôs enter the
second half of this decade confident that every problem before us can be
tackled with the tools in our hands.
Guided by our values, we‚Äôll use those tools expeditiously‚Äîand
wisely‚Äîbecause whatever else South Bend is, above all, South Bend is home.
